   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d75216575" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Mn-rich layered oxide cathodes with anionic redox promise high energy density for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to ultra-high capacity derived from both Mn and O redox couples. Nevertheless, instability of the reactions that lead to poor electrochemical stability hinders the cathodes from practical applications. Here, the Al and Zn dual-site substitution strategy is proposed to enhance electrochemical performance. The designed cathode, Na<sub>0.73</sub>Zn<sub>0.03</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>Al<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (AlZn), delivers a high discharge capacity of 242 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an impressive rate capability (162 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and excellent capacity retention (89.69% over 150 cycles). In addition, full-cell SIB based on AlZn coupled with hard carbon exhibits a high energy density of 317 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on both cathode and anode mass) and a reasonable capacity retention of 80.8% after 250 cycles. Revealed by advanced investigations, the synergy of robust Al–O in TM layers and O–Zn–O pillars in Na layers helps alleviate severe inactive spinel/rock-salt phase transformation and intragranular cracks in the AlZn cathode. This consequently leads to greatly enhanced electrochemical performance over the pristine cathode. This work provides insight into improving electrochemical properties of anionic-redox-based layered oxides by Al/Zn co-substitutions toward high-energy SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411928-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411928-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Ubiquitous lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been recently confronting inevitable limitations of pricing, geopolitical, and environmental concerns of main cathode materials (Li, Ni, and Co) restricting further sustainable development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0001_R_d75216567e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a consequence, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have regained a great deal of attention as a promising alternative toward satisfying escalating demand for energy storage systems with more sustainability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0002_R_d75216567e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is principally owing to the intrinsic advantages of Na such as high earth-abundance leading to low cost of resources, more environmental benign, similarity in energy-storage chemistry and manufacturing to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0003_R_d75216567e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0004_R_d75216567e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This manifests a great potential for SIBs as the next front-runner rechargeable batteries in the future. Albeit the merits, SIBs still suffer from low energy density mainly limited by intrinsically electrochemical properties of sodium-based cathode materials such as heavy atomic weight, large ion size, and high standard electrochemical reduction potential resulting in low operating cell voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0005_R_d75216567e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These could lead to high cost per Wh of SIB cells, which consequently limits a variety of practical implementations, compared to competitive LIBs. Since cathode plays a pivotal role in determining energy density of battery cells, numerous efforts have been devoted to developing novel cathode chemistries for SIBs with high energy density. Na-based layered oxide cathodes with anionic O redox chemistry promise high energy density since they can provide high capacity beyond theoretical capacity activated by redox reactions of lattice O (O<sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup>) at high voltages, in addition to sole utilization of cationic redox reactions. Main paradigms of Na cathodes that involve the anionic redox are Na-rich (Na &gt; 1) compounds such as Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>IrO<sub>3</sub>, and P2-type Na-deficient (Na &lt; 1) compounds incorporated with Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup> and vacancy (□) such as Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mg<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.72</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Cu<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.72</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.72</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>4/7</sub>[□<sub>1/7</sub>Mn<sub>6/7</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0006_R_d75216567e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Joule">6</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0013_R_d75216567e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these, the Na-deficient cathode compositions with Li are of the most interest due to their highest specific energy (&gt;700 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), which is much higher than their cousin compositions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0006_R_d75216567e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Joule">6</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0014_R_d75216567e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This extraordinary high energy is resulted from partial substitution of Li<sup>+</sup> in transition metal (TM) sites to generate a Na–O–Li local configuration that can easily invoke O redox couples due to the presence of nonbonding O 2p slightly below the Fermi level, in addition to cationic Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox couples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0015_R_d75216567e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the excellent capacity, it is inevitably accompanied by severe structural degradation, which is caused by lattice O loss at high voltage regions as well as Jahn-Teller distortion induced by high-spin Mn<sup>3+</sup> at low voltage regions during electrochemical reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0017_R_d75216567e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0018_R_d75216567e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These major problems cause serious deterioration of electrochemical performance after a number of cycles posing a great challenge for the anionic-redox-based and Mn-rich types layered oxides in practical utilizations.</p>
         
         <p>Modifications of pristine cathode materials with various strategies such as substitutions with different elements, surface coating, and surface doping have been widely proposed to alleviate the aforementioned problems of Mn- and anionic-redox-based layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0019_R_d75216567e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Of most interest, substituting or doping electrochemically active/inert species with different valences into transition metal (TM) sites such as F<sup>−</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, Sn<sup>4+</sup>, Zr<sup>4+</sup>, Si<sup>4+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup> to regulate local structures have been proven to enhance structural stability upon long cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0014_R_d75216567e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0018_R_d75216567e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0020_R_d75216567e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0022_R_d75216567e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to TM-site substitutions, incorporating elements into Na sites also proves to greatly stabilize the cathode structure. Li<sup>+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup> ions were used to substitute Na<sup>+</sup> sites acting as immobile pillars to stabilize the host structure during high state of charge (SoC).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0023_R_d75216567e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0026_R_d75216567e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the substituting strategies mostly enhance cycling stability of the pristine cathodes, there is a tradeoff between decreased capacity and enhanced stability. Hence, searching for elements that avoid largely lowering initial capacity and simultaneously improve cycling stability is challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we present a dual-site substitution of Al and Zn into P2-type Mn-based Na<sub>0.73</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (PR) cathode to improve the electrochemical performance. The Na<sub>0.73</sub>Zn<sub>0.03</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>Al<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (AlZn) cathode material is newly designed in this work. In the Na-metal half cells, this AlZn cathode delivers a high reversible capacity of 242 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C = 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) which is comparable to that of PR (248 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The modified cathode delivers a high capacity of 162 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C as well as an excellent capacity retention of 89.69% over 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. To demonstrate feasibility of practical utilization, full-cell SIBs with this AlZn cathode and hard carbon anode are fabricated. The full cell exhibits a high specific energy of 317 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on mass of cathode and anode) with a reasonable cycling stability of 80.8% after 250 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. Revealed by combined experimental and computational results, the greatly enhanced electrochemical performance stems from well-retained lattice O redox and ameliorated Jahn-Teller distortion from Mn redox after introducing Al and Zn into the host cathode. At a high SoC, where anionic redox is mainly invoked, the presences of Al in TM layers and Zn in Na layers serving as stable strong pillars help alleviate severe layered-to-spinel/rock-salt phase transformation and coincident intragranular cracks that jeopardize electrochemical stability. Altogether, this work provides a novel design of layered oxides with high energy density and stability, which is a promising cathode for high performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411928-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411928-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202411928-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202411928-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>A designed P2-type Na<sub>0.73</sub>Zn<sub>0.03</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>Al<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode was successfully synthesized via a high-temperature solid-state reaction. Inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) confirms that the compositions of targeted AlZn sample are close to the designed stoichiometry. To gain information on crystal structures of the as-prepared materials, X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) analysis was performed, as depicted in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). All the synthesized samples show a typical P2-type structure in a <i>P6<sub>3</sub></i> space group with a distinct honeycomb-type superstructure at 2θ ≈ 21. Of note, the superstructure is typically seen in Na-Li-Mn oxides due to in-plane Li/Mn ordering in transition metal (TM) layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0024_R_d75216567e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0027_R_d75216567e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Crystallographic information of all samples acquired by XRD Rietveld refinement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1b,c</a>) is summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The changes in lattice parameters indicate successful substitutions of Al and Zn into the layered oxide structure. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was employed to reveal morphology as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Both PR and AlZn possess a hexagonal-plate-like morphology with particle sizes of ≈10 µm, further confirmed by particle size distribution analysis (PSD) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). It is seen that AlZn possesses a slightly smaller particle size compared to PR. This consequently leads to a slightly higher surface area of AlZn, according to Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area analysis in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). It is worth noting that the smaller particles and higher surface area of AlZn can provide more active areas contacting electrolytes, thereby improving electrochemical properties. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1d</a> also shows the plate-like morphology of AlZn. Besides, high-angle annular dark-filed scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-TEM) was employed to reveal the cathode structure in atomic scale. According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1e</a>, it can be seen that Zn atoms locate in the Na layers reflected by the bright spots in the yellow boxes. Note that Na and O cannot be seen in the HAADF due to their low atomic mass.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0024_R_d75216567e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The location of Zn atoms in Na layers was previously observed in P2-type Na<sub>0.83</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes by Zhang et al and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes by Peng et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0023_R_d75216567e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0028_R_d75216567e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was validated that Zn atoms preferably locate in Na layers due to thermodynamic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0023_R_d75216567e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For Al<sup>3+</sup> ions, they substitute Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the transition metal slabs due to their similar ionic radii.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0029_R_d75216567e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1f</a> shows a typical hexagonal layered structure of this cathode. Furthermore, to further illustrate the preferred location of Zn atoms, formation energy of Al/Zn co-substituted cathodes with Zn in TM and Na layers as well as the pristine sample was calculated. The formation energy in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1g</a> reveals that the cathode with Zn in Na layers possesses the lowest formation energy indicating that Zn is prone to residing in Na layers. Also, the formation energy is much lower than that of pristine cathode (PR) implying more structural stability of AlZn.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/afeb30e9-cd0e-4aca-8b63-f16cc27f90b0/smll202411928-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/afeb30e9-cd0e-4aca-8b63-f16cc27f90b0/smll202411928-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/344bb381-c949-44df-9467-59aac2c5ddef/smll202411928-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/afeb30e9-cd0e-4aca-8b63-f16cc27f90b0/smll202411928-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Materials characterizations: a) XRD pattern of as-synthesized PR and AlZn samples, the Rietveld refinement of b) PR and c) AlZn, d) TEM image, e) HAADF image, f) corresponding SAED pattern. g) Calculated formation energy of pristine cathode and cathodes with Zn-doped in Na and TM layers. h) TEM-EDS mapping of the AlZn sample.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, TEM-EDS mappings are employed to reveal elemental distribution in the AlZn sample. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0001">1h</a>, it shows that Al distributes homogeneously throughout the particle while Zn seemingly locates in the bulk as the signals of Zn are stronger than the surface. This is also confirmed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results of Al 2p and Zn 2p, shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Al<sup>3+</sup> peak can be observed at both surface and sub-surface (after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 120 s) of the sample while Zn<sup>2+</sup> peak can only be detected at sub-surface. Also, XPS spectra of Mn 2p shows that there is an increase of Mn<sup>4+</sup> after incorporating Al<sup>3+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup> into the cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The higher fraction of high-spin Mn<sup>3+</sup> could induce severe Jahn-Teller distortion during cycling especially to low voltage regions, thereby deteriorating electrochemical stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0023_R_d75216567e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, Al/Zn substitution could help mitigate this problem, which will be investigated by electrochemical studies.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202411928-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202411928-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Investigation of Half-Cell Sodium-Ion Batteries</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical properties of synthesized cathodes were investigated with various electrochemical tests within a potential range of 1.5–4.5V in a half-cell configuration with Na metal anode at room temperature. Note that the cutoff potential at 4.5&nbsp;V is the optimal range to avoid excessive electrolyte decomposition which severely happens when charging to 4.6&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). Room temperature is also selected for all the electrochemical property tests to avoid risks from electrolyte decompositions at a high temperature at 55&nbsp;°C. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information). The first-cycle charge/discharge profile at 0.1&nbsp;C of each sample obtained from galvanostatic charge/discharge technique (GCD) is presented in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information). As seen, the charge/discharge curve characteristics are identical for all samples in which a long plateau at high voltages (&gt;4.0 to 4.5&nbsp;V), typically related to lattice O oxidation (O<sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup>), are solely observed during the initial charge process. The is followed by a slopping characteristic of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> reduction at low voltage regions during the subsequent discharge process. The first discharge capacity of PR yields 248 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> while the single Al-substituted (Al) and Zn-substituted (Zn) samples exhibit their first discharge capacities of 252 and 225 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It can be noticed that the discrepancy in fist cycle capacity implies the different effects of single-Al/Zn substitution on electrochemical properties. According to the 1st cycle dQ/dV plot in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), with sole Al substitution, the anionic redox activity is the highest amongst others. This indicates that Al could promote the anionic redox activity in layered oxide cathodes leading to its highest capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0030_R_d75216567e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small Methods">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the only Zn-substituted sample shows the lowest anionic redox activity and its onset O oxidation potential is farer than others, indicating that O is more difficultly oxidized at such high voltage due to strengthened O framework in the layered structure from the robust O–Zn–O pillars in the Na layers. Therefore, this could be a reason why the Zn-doped sample possesses the lowest capacity. With Al/Zn co-substitution, the AlZn sample delivers its first discharge capacity of 242 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which is trivially lower than that of PR. Rate capability was also evaluated at different current densities (C-rate) ranging from 0.1 to 5&nbsp;C to emphasize the effect of Al and Zn co-substitution. As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), AlZn possesses the best rate retention among others where a large capacity of 162 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is gained at a high rate of 5C, accounting for 67.63% of its initial capacity at 0.1&nbsp;C. Meanwhile, PR is able to maintain only 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C, which is 49.22% retention. The comparative dQ/dV plots of PR and AlZn at 5C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2c</a> reveal that the presence of anionic-redox feature as well as more reversible Mn redox in AlZn are much more pronounced than in PR even at such a high rate. Hence, the impressive rate capability of AlZn could be due to the well-retained Mn and O redoxes at high rates. It is worth noting that the achieved high capacity at 5C is one of the highest amongst state-of-the-art P2-type anionic-based cathodes, to our best knowledge, as summarized in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). Owing to the excellent capacity and potential retentions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2e</a>) of AlZn at high rates, the modified cathode delivers a high specific energy of 409.43 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C (61.93% retention), as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2f</a>. This is much &gt;288.25 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C of PR, only 43.62% retention. Moreover, cycling stability after prolonged cycling of all the cathodes was also investigated at 1&nbsp;C for 150 cycles, which is depicted in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2g</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> (Supporting Information). With single Al and Zn substitution, the cycling stabilities (80.59% for Al and 91.07% for Zn) are improved over the PR sample (79.89%) due to rigid Al–O and O–Zn–O pillars in respective TM and Na layers. Benefited by the synergy of Al and Zn, impressively, AlZn also offers excellent capacity retention of 89.69% which is superior to that of PR sample while possessing the highest capacity amongst others at 1C. Considering PR and AlZn, as observed in the charge/discharge profiles from 2nd cycle to 150th cycle at 1&nbsp;C in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,c</a> (Supporting Information), the potential and capacity fading are much smaller for AlZn than those of PR. This is also revealed by less Mn-redox peak shift and highly reversible O redox activity shown in the dQ/dV plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b,d</a>, Supporting Information). Also, the higher Mn oxidation reactivity after several cycles and depletion of O oxidation reactivity in PR indicate more Jahn–Teller distortion effect induced by Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the PR cathode leading to poorer electrochemical performance. From these results, it is attributed that the Al and Zn co-substitution alleviates irreversible Mn- and O-redox reactions upon prolonged cycling, thereby greatly enhancing the cycling stability of the cathode. Other than that, AlZn also outperforms PR in specific energy retention for 150 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2h</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/384a0658-5e4f-44aa-a725-dd03909b5061/smll202411928-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/384a0658-5e4f-44aa-a725-dd03909b5061/smll202411928-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0f4142e2-5a8b-43e4-a099-43fca169f743/smll202411928-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/384a0658-5e4f-44aa-a725-dd03909b5061/smll202411928-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) 1st cycle charge/discharge profiles of PR and AlZn. b) Rate capability. c) dQ/dV plots at 5C. d) Capacities at high current rates (&gt;200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) in half-cell of AlZn cathode compared to other anionic-based layered oxide sodium-ion battery cathodes. e) Potential retention at different C-rates. f) Specific energy retention at different C-rates. g) Capacity retention at 1C for 150 cycles. h) Specific energy retention at 1C for 150 cycles. CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> for 10 cycles of i) PR and j) AlZn. CV curves at different scan rates of k) PR and l) AlZn.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was also employed to elucidate the improved electrochemical properties of the AlZn sample. CV curves of PR and AlZn shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2i,j</a> exhibit two main Mn and O redox couples, Mn redox at low voltages (&lt;2.5V) and O redox at high voltages (&gt;4.0V) related to the charge/discharge profiles. Upon repeatedly scanning for 10 cycles, the shift of Mn oxidation peak at 2.0&nbsp;V toward higher potential during charging (desodiation) accompanied by depletion of O oxidation at ≈4.2&nbsp;V is clearly observed in both samples. This is caused by the compensation of irreversible lattice O loss in which it is more pronounced in PR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0020_R_d75216567e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharging (sodiation), greater reversible Mn and O redoxes can be seen in the AlZn sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). This observation from CV is consistent with the aforementioned dQ/dV plots after several cycles, confirming that the modification can enhance Mn- and O-redox reversibility upon electrochemical cycling leading to superior electrochemical performance of AlZn. Moreover, CV at different scan rates were performed to investigate the rate performance of the cathodes through the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0002">2k,l</a> depicts the CV curves of PR and AlZn at scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As witnessed, the CV curves provide an insignificant difference at low scan rates between the two samples. Upon increasing scan rates, the O oxidation peak (O2) of AlZn distinctly exhibits a higher current intensity than that of PR. This implies higher O anionic redox participation at high current rates which contributes to improved rate capability in AlZn. It could be attributed that AlZn possesses greater Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation kinetics at high rates than PR. To reveal Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in both PR and AlZn, a plot of log(I<sub>p</sub>) vs. log(v) is conducted as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). The slope values derived from the linear relationship imply charge storage mechanisms of the cathode materials. If the slope values are close to 0.5, it is indicative of Faradaic-intercalation process. If the values are near 1.0, the charge-storage mechanism is controlled by pseudo-capacitive behavior. The slope values of both samples are around 0.6–0.9, indicating that there is a combination of two mechanisms in these cathode materials. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,b</a> (Supporting Information), the slope values of PR and AlZn have no distinct discrepancy at the low voltages (O1 and R1). In contrast, the slope values of O2 and R2 are obviously higher in AlZn when increasing the scan rates, illustrating more rapid reaction kinetics of AlZn at high voltages facilitated by more pseudo-capacitive behavior. Moreover, the O2/O1 ratio in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) shows that AlZn possesses higher values indicating more O oxidation is maintained at high rates. Also, the less Mn oxidation activity (O1) and high O oxidation activity (O2) in the charging process at high rates implies that Jahn-Teller distortion induced by Mn<sup>3+</sup> and lattice O loss are effectively alleviated in the AlZn sample.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the electrochemical investigations, it manifests that the Al/Zn dual-site substitution can greatly improve the overall electrochemical performance of Mn-rich and anionic-redox-based layered oxides. This is synergically benefited by promoting high anionic redox activity from Al substitution and the greatly enhanced structural stability from Zn substitution. This impressive performance exhibits a great possibility for this material to be implemented in high-energy-density and high-power-density applications.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202411928-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202411928-sec-0050-title">2.3 Structural Evaluation of Cathodes in Half-Cell Sodium-Ion Batteries</h3>
            
            <p>Based on the notable difference in electrochemical performances of the two samples, the effects of Al/Zn dual-substitutions on structural evolution were further investigated. As charging to high voltages to exploit the anionic redox is vulnerable to structural collapse of cathode materials due to the excessive desodiation accompanied by inevitable cation migration and severe side reactions, TEM at a 4.5V-charged state at 15th cycle was performed to reveal structural stability at the high SoC of both cathodes, which is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3a,d</a>. In general, we can observe particle cracks in both samples which could possibly be the reason of capacity degradation in the layered oxide cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0031_R_d75216567e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Energy Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, larger intragranular cracks and voids are witnessed in the pristine cathode. With the presence of Al and Zn in the structure, there are fewer and smaller crack openings in the AlZn cathode ensuring improved structural stability. To probe the structural change in atomic scale, high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) at the near-crack regions was carried out for both cathodes as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3b,e</a>. Obviously, electrochemically inactive spinel/rock-salt phase are present significantly in the PR sample after cycling showing a significant structural disorder. Meanwhile, less disordered structure is seen in the modified cathode. It is reported that those disordered phases are caused by irreversible cation migration from TM layers to Na layers and lattice O loss at a high state of charge, normally occurring in high-voltage-based layered oxide cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0024_R_d75216567e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that the phase transformation from layered to spinel/rock-salt phases possibly leads to such particle cracks due to uneven internal stress distribution. Therefore, the strong Al─O bond in TM layers and O–Zn–O in Na layers that act as rigid pillars c an lessen the cation migration and strengthen the lattice O in the layered structure to form the inactive phases at such a deeply desodiated state. As a consequence, the layered structure is more greatly stabilized in AlZn, resulting in better electrochemical retention. Geometric phase analysis (GPA) was correspondingly carried out to visualize internal stress distribution around the cracks. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3c,f</a> showing a color mapping of the two electrodes, it is obvious that heterogeneous internal stress distribution can be distinctly seen in the PR sample, which indicates severe lattice distortion. The inhomogeneous internal stress concentration caused by formations of inactive spinel-/rock-salt-like phases consequently induces particle intragranular cracks and eventual layered structure collapse. Note that such crack openings can accelerate electrochemical degradation by facilitating HF and H<sub>2</sub>O attack, then bringing about severe side reactions and loss of active elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0020_R_d75216567e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0022_R_d75216567e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0032_R_d75216567e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Controversy, the AlZn sample witnesses more homogeneous internal stress distribution in the region of interest, indicating less lattice distortion. From these results, it reveals that the uneven distribution of internal stress caused by harsh layered to spinel/rock-salt phase transformation implies an origin of large capacity decay upon long cycling in PR. The problem can be alleviated by the proposed Al and Zn dual-site substitution strategy. Moreover, electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) was also conducted for both samples to reveal Mn and O redox related to structural evolution at the 4.5V-charged state. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a,c</a> (Supporting Information), compared to AlZn, there are more obvious changes in O K-edge spectra from the near crack regions to bulk in PR. The intensity of pre peak is significantly weak in PR implying a high degree of lattice O loss in the crack regions. The difference in energy (ΔE) of the pre-peak and main peak in O K-edge spectra can also be used to evaluate the lattice O evolution at the cracks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0033_R_d75216567e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0034_R_d75216567e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Commun.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be seen that ΔE in PR is obviously deviated from the bulk-area to the crack regions. In contrast, such a deviation is significantly less in AlZn. The lower value of ΔE at the crack regions signals the reduction of TM<sub>3d</sub>–O<sub>2p</sub> hybridization and loss of lattice O. Hence, this manifests that PR undergoes a more severe reduction of TM–O covalency and lattice O loss concomitantly forming the disordered phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0017_R_d75216567e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0033_R_d75216567e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0035_R_d75216567e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the better-maintained lattice O results can be seen in the AlZn sample, which is consistent with the fewer formation of inactive phases and cracks, resulting in higher anionic redox activity in AlZn. The well-maintained high anionic redox activity stemming from reinforced lattice O can be observed by ex situ XPS spectra of O 1s in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) in which the signal of peroxo-like species (O<sup>n−</sup>) at 530.1 eV is more intense than that of PR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0036_R_d75216567e1080" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Energy Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mn L-edge EELS spectra of both samples were also investigated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b,d</a> (Supporting Information). There is an observable shift to lower energy of Mn L<sub>3</sub> peak near the crack regions which indicates the reduced valence states of Mn from Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>(3+δ)+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0037_R_d75216567e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Commun.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the deviation of Mn L<sub>3</sub> peak from the cracks to the bulk structure, the feature is obviously seen in the PR sample while a slight deviation is witnessed in AlZn. This indicates the larger formation of Mn<sup>(3+δ)+</sup> species in the PR cathode, which is related to the significant formation of spinel/rock-salt phases. This is also consistent with the more obvious shift to lower binding energy at the charged state of ex situ XPS spectra of Mn 2p as well as larger Mn<sup>3+</sup> proportion, shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, the higher fraction of Mn<sup>3+</sup> at the charge state in PR also indicates the Mn reduction (Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>) to compensate loss of lattice O redox.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0029_R_d75216567e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0038_R_d75216567e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Storage Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent cycles, more Mn oxidation (Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>) is induced, resulting in more Jahn-Teller distortion that degrades the cathode structure and electrochemical performance. This is evidenced by higher Mn oxidation and lower O oxidation after several cycles in the PR sample in the electrochemical tests. Hence, these atomic-level findings validate that the phase transformation from layered structure to electrochemically inactive disordered phases accordingly inducing intragranular cracks lead to substantially degraded electrochemical performance in PR. This drawback can be ameliorated with a synergy of Al and Zn substituted in respective TM and Na layers through robust pillar effects, consequently improving electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cccc1df6-7a2a-43e2-ac0e-a6c4e995f181/smll202411928-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cccc1df6-7a2a-43e2-ac0e-a6c4e995f181/smll202411928-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/83fdd94e-18ac-4ce7-a8f4-a4957d0cb3c0/smll202411928-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cccc1df6-7a2a-43e2-ac0e-a6c4e995f181/smll202411928-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TEM image, b) HR-TEM images, and c) corresponding GPA analysis at 15th charged cycle of PR. d) TEM image, e) HR-TEM images, and f) corresponding GPA analysis at 15th charged cycle of AlZn. Post-mortem analysis after 60 cycles: SEM images of g) PR and h) AlZn, i) Nyquist plot obtained from EIS measurement (inset is enlarged high-frequency area), j) Raman spectra of cycled electrodes, and GITT measurement of k) charged cycle and l) discharged cycle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Structural stability of the cathodes after prolonged cycles was further evaluated. SEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3g</a> obviously shows that large crack openings and planar gildings can be seen in the PR sample. In contrast, AlZn maintains greater structural stability after such a long cycling process as much smaller cracks and gildings are seen in the cathode particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3h</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was also conducted at a discharge state to investigate the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and charge transfer resistance of the cathodes after being cycled. The equivalent circuit for fitting and fitting results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The Nyquist plot shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3i</a> illustrates a lower solid-state-electrolyte interface resistance (R<sub>SEI</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) after the prolonged cycling indicating thinner SEI layer formation and less inactive spinel and rock-salt phases in the AlZn structure. It is worth noting that such inactive phases can increase charge transfer resistance of the cathode limiting Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0032_R_d75216567e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectroscopy within 400–800 cm<sup>−1</sup> was also adopted to evaluate the structural reversibility of the layered oxide cathodes after cycling since it is sensitive to the local structures of cathodes. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), before cycling, there are two main bands in which E<sub>2g</sub> band at 480 cm<sup>−1</sup> refers to a symmetric stretching of Na–O and A<sub>g</sub> band at 580 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to TM–O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0039_R_d75216567e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0041_R_d75216567e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nat. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After 60 cycles, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3j</a>, the Raman spectrum of PR shows a new band at ≈630 cm<sup>−1</sup> which is related to formation of distorted phases such as spinel-phase domain in the PR sample while it is not distinctly observed in AlZn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0040_R_d75216567e1204" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Storage Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#smll202411928-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0042_R_d75216567e1204" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the Al/Zn co-substituted sample has greater structural reversibility than the pristine cathode upon cycling for a long time. To probe the effect of structural distortion on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) at different cycles was tested as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3k,l</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), it is seen that PR and AlZn have comparable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) at initial 4th cycle. However, D<sub>Na+</sub> of AlZn after the long cycling of 60 cycles is obviously higher than that of PR, as can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0003">3k,l</a>. It is attributed to alleviated inactive-phase formation and well-preserved structural integrity in the Al/Zn-doped sample, leading to improved electrochemical kinetics after cycles. This also indicates that a higher degree of inactive and irreversible phase transformation in PR prevents effective Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion leading to its inferior rate retention and prolonged cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0032_R_d75216567e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0043_R_d75216567e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, ex situ XRD after a long cycling of 150 cycles was conducted to evaluate the structural stability. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), it can be seen that AlZn possesses higher intensity and narrower full width at half maximum (FWHM) of (002) peak 2θ&nbsp;≈ 16°. This is indicative of the well-maintained long-range-ordered layered structure even after the prolonged cycles in AlZn, supporting the superior electrochemical performance after the Al/Zn modification.</p>
            
            <p>An illustration in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0004">4</a> summarizes the effects of Al and Zn dual-substitutions on improving electrochemical properties of anionic-based layered oxides. During cycling to a high SoC at 4.5&nbsp;V, lattice O oxidation is mainly activated accompanied by inevitable Li and Mn migration, consequently causing structural degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0044_R_d75216567e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0045_R_d75216567e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nature">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the weak Li─O and Mn─O bonds in PR, the excessive O oxidation leads to lattice O loss, Mn reduction inducing Mn<sup>3+</sup> Jahn-Teller distortion, and severe phase transformations from layered to inactive spinel/rock-salt phases. The inhomogenous phases in the PR finally cause distorted layered structures and intragranular cracks in the cathode particles. Note that the crack openings could facilitate the H<sub>2</sub>O and HF attacks which can accelerate side reactions, loss of active species, increase charge transfer resistance and ultimately degrade electrochemical performance. This is due to the lack of structural stabilizers when charging to the high voltage, consequently causing poor electrochemical performance in PR. In contrast, with the presence of Al<sup>3+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup>, these introduced atoms serve as robust Al–O and O–Zn–O pillar stabilizers at respective TM layers and Na layers, which reinforce the two slabs at such a deep desodiation stage. Hence, the layered structure is well maintained, and such a harsh formation of inactive spinel/rock-salt phases and intragranular cracks are effectively ameliorated in AlZn, therefore substantially enhancing electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a963e2da-2a19-4bf6-b2af-9d904c4354d2/smll202411928-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a963e2da-2a19-4bf6-b2af-9d904c4354d2/smll202411928-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc5f3f1b-e316-4626-9760-22eb973002d2/smll202411928-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a963e2da-2a19-4bf6-b2af-9d904c4354d2/smll202411928-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration to summarize the function of Al/Zn dual-site substitution when being charged to at 4.5V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202411928-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202411928-sec-0060-title">2.4 DFT Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>Projected density of states (PDOS) of O 2p and Mn 3d were additionally calculated using DFT to emphasize the effects of Al/Zn co-substitution on enhancing electrochemical properties through electronic band structures, as revealed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0005">5a,b</a>. Compared to PR, AlZn possesses a larger proportion of isolated O 2p near the Fermi energy (E<sub>f</sub>) at the same energy level, implying that more O redox can be triggered and better charge transfer of O redox especially at high rates. The observation is similarly seen in Al-doped anionic-based layered oxides in which doped Al atoms elevate O 2p states to be closer to the E<sub>f,</sub> thereby boosting the anionic redox in layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0016_R_d75216567e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0030_R_d75216567e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small Methods">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, this could be a validation of retained high capacity in AlZn after introducing such electrochemically-inactive species. Moreover, the states of Mn 3d after introducing Al and Zn are mainly distributed farther below E<sub>f</sub>, which is indicative of a greater likelihood of Mn<sup>4+</sup>(t<sup>3</sup><sub>2g</sub> e<sup>0</sup><sub>g</sub>) than Mn<sup>3+</sup> (t<sup>3</sup><sub>2g</sub> e<sup>1</sup><sub>g</sub>) in the structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0023_R_d75216567e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0046_R_d75216567e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Mater.">46</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0047_R_d75216567e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Lett.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In obvious contrast, Mn 3d predominantly occupies states in the vicinity of E<sub>f</sub> in the PR sample. This reveals that there is a larger proportion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the pristine cathode, which can potentially induce more Jahn-Teller distortion during electrochemical cycling and accelerate structural degradation. This is good agreement with the experimental findings.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/79119c6d-21b3-4af8-97d9-1b1314624aca/smll202411928-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/79119c6d-21b3-4af8-97d9-1b1314624aca/smll202411928-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/41d7b379-cad5-4425-be18-2f606708d1c3/smll202411928-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/79119c6d-21b3-4af8-97d9-1b1314624aca/smll202411928-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">PDOS of O 2p and Mn 3d of a) PR and b) AlZn. c) MSD curves at different temperatures. d) Arrhenius plots of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to further investigate the effect on sodium-ion diffusion after modification, <i>Ab initio</i> molecular dynamics simulation (AIMD) was also performed. According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0005">5c</a>, the mean square displacements (MSD) of Na<sup>+</sup> are larger for AlZn compared to PR indicative of more Na<sup>+</sup> migrations and better Na<sup>+</sup> mobility under the similar simulation time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0028_R_d75216567e1373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This also implies that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path is more interconnected with the presence of Zn in the Na layers, which results in the more effective Na<sup>+</sup> mobility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0028_R_d75216567e1382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the activation energy barrier (E<sub>a</sub>) can be calculated from the slope of Arrhenius plot as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0005">5d</a> to investigate the activation energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ion hopping between adjacent prismatic sites in the P2-type cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0048_R_d75216567e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Sci. Adv.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is seen that AlZn has lower E<sub>a</sub> (3.720 eV) than PR (5.398 eV), indicating that the reduced E<sub>a</sub> facilitates fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport in the AlZn cathode. This is because the introduction of Zn ions in Na layers disrupts Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and thereby decreases the diffusion energy barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411928-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0048_R_d75216567e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Sci. Adv.">48</a>, <a href="#smll202411928-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411928-bib-0049_R_d75216567e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The prediction of D<sub>Na+</sub> at 300K deduced from the Arrhenius plot also shows that AlZn possesses a higher value of 1.16×10<sup>−11</sup> while PR owns a lower value of 1.1×10<sup>−13</sup>, further illustrating a better Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of the modified cathode. In addition, the immobile Zn atoms can help preserve the layered structure during Na<sup>+</sup> extracting and prevent the structural collapse, resulting in effective Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion even after several cycling. Altogether, these computational results agree well with the greatly enhanced rate capability and overall electrochemical properties of the modified cathode as revealed by various experimental results.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202411928-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202411928-sec-0070-title">2.5 Fabrication of High-Performance Full-Cell Sodium-Ion Batteries</h3>
            
            <p>Inspired by the excellent electrochemical performance in the half-cell system, the AlZn cathode is coupled with hard carbon anode to fabricate a full-cell sodium-ion battery, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202411928-fig-0006">6a</a>, to evaluate the performance in a more practical aspect. The electrochemical performance of hard carbon anode tested in a half cell with its Na metal counterpart is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). It delivers a reversible capacity of 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a reasonable rate and cycling performance. Nonetheless, the hard carbon anode typically suffers from severe irreversible capacity for sodium-ion storage due to severe solid-state electrolyte interface (SEI) formation. Hence, prior to use, the hard carbon is pre-sodiated with Na metal to alleviate the problems. To balance capacity, the negative-to-positive capacity (N/P) ratio used in this work is 1.2–1.3. The operating voltage window used in the full cell is controlled between 1.4 and 4.4&nbsp;V. The voltage profile of sodium-ion battery full cell is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0006">6b</a>. It shows an identical charge/discharge characteristic curves to those in half-cell battery. However, the first discharge capacity of full cell is only 210 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on cathode mass), which is lower than 242 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> achieved in the half-cell system. This is possibly caused by the aforementioned limitations of hard carbon anode resulting in lower discharge capacity at the initial cycle. The full cell outputs an average cell voltage of 2.76V consequently delivering a high specific energy of 580 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on cathode mass) and 317 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on both cathode and anode mass). Rate capability at different C rates is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0006">6c</a>. It is seen that the full cell can deliver a high capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C. Nevertheless, the capacity is lower than that of half-cell SIB at the same C-rate. This lowered capacity at the high C-rate is caused by sluggish Na diffusion in hard carbon anode at high C-rates that results in poor rate capability. The full-cell battery achieves 80.08% of capacity retention after 250 cycles at 1C with a stable average Coulombic efficiency of 99.08%, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411928-fig-0006">6d</a>. This indicates excellent stability of this cathode in the full-cell system, in addition to the half-cell system. To our best knowledge, the full-cell performance records the highest specific energy amongst other reported sodium-ion cathodes as well as excellent cycling stability as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). This is indicative of a great potential cathode toward high energy density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202411928-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6115cc83-db62-4aab-b4d3-3471b69c14c7/smll202411928-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6115cc83-db62-4aab-b4d3-3471b69c14c7/smll202411928-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c006db39-49d5-42d7-a061-47a76361f86e/smll202411928-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6115cc83-db62-4aab-b4d3-3471b69c14c7/smll202411928-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411928-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411928" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) A diagram of full-cell sodium-ion battery with AlZn cathode and hard carbon anode. b) Initial three cycle voltage profiles of the full cell. c) Rate capability at different current densities. d) Cycling performance for 250 cycles of the full cell.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Altogether, the designed AlZn cathode provides excellent electrochemical performance in both half- and full-cell SIBs in terms of energy density, rate capability, and cycling stability showing a great potential for being implemented in next-generation Na-metal and conventional rechargeable Na-based batteries for high energy density applications.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411928-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411928-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, P2-type Na<sub>0.73</sub>Zn<sub>0.03</sub>Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>Al<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode was successfully synthesized by a facile solid-state reaction at a high temperature for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The cathode delivers a highly reversible discharge capacity of 242 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to a high specific energy 660.73 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on cathode mass. Also, a high capacity of 162 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved at a high current rate of 1,000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> as well as good cycling stability of 89.69% for 150 cycles. In addition, the full-cell SIB with AlZn cathode delivers a high specific energy of 317 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on cathode and anode mass offering a great potential cathode for high-energy SIBs. Unraveled by material, electrochemical, and DFT computational investigations, the presences of Al and Zn in the cathode structure can improve the electrochemical properties by retaining high lattice O redox activity and reducing Mn<sup>3+</sup> that can induce Jahn–Teller distortion during cycling. Although utilizing anionic redox at high voltages is vulnerable to structural degradation, the stable Al─O bond in TM and O–Zn–O in Na layers in the modified sample can lessen the phase transformation from layered structures to disordered spinel/rock-salt structures and concomitant intragranular cracks leading to more structural integrity and well-maintained electrochemical properties. Altogether, not only can this work propose a novel strategy to enhance electrochemical performance of Mn-rich and anionic-based cathode toward the future development of high-energy sodium-ion batteries but also sheds light on the effects of Al/Zn dual-site substitution on improving electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
